B005KMX866|4.0|Cover albums can be a tricky thing, especially when the singer is particularly known for their ability as a songwriter. I think this is why Chris Isaak's last (partial) album of cover tunes, 1996's BAJA SESSIONS, is still my least favorite Isaak disc...it just doesn't represent him the way his other albums do.<br />  Needless to say, I was a little worried when I found out that he was doing a full album's worth (or two...I went with the deluxe two disc set!) of Sun studio classics....after recent cover projects from the likes of Cyndi Lauper and Huey Lewis and the News, a visit to Karaokeville was a real concern.<br />  Well, I'm happy to report that nothing could be furthr from that end result...BEYOND THE SUN is an absolute winner on every level! Not only does the album feature Isaak's best vocals to date, but he manages to honor these classics while still making them his own. (In fact, if you didn't know better, you would swear many of these songs were Isaak originals!).<br />  Since there's so much material to cover, and I don't want to short change anything, I'm going to do something different with this reveiw...rather than going deep into each track, I'm just going to do bullet points on each one, highlighting the things that really jumped out at me each time. Let's go!<br />  \\"Ring Of Fire\\" ~ Opening seconds are close to a Johnny Cash rip-off, but by song's end Isaak rights things, comfortably wearing this Cash classic.<br />  \\"Trying To Get To You\\" ~ A totally boppin' lead vocal, killer guitars, SO smooth!<br />  \\"I Forgot To Remember To Forget\\" ~ Romantic, yet spirited. Great arrangement. (And I'll tell you right now..watch out for piano player Scotty Plunkett...trust me, you've been warned!).<br />  \\"Great Balls Of Fire\\" ~ Covers of this song usually sound SO cheesy, but Isaak just NAILS it! Loose, lively, relaxed...the track's AMAZING! One of the disc's best vocal, it's rivaled only by Plunkett's playing. Seriously...he almost steals the show right out from under Isaak, both here and elsewhere throughout the disc. Abso-frickin'-lutely brilliant!<br />  \\"Can't Help Falling In Love\\" ~ At first you think this is nothing more than a standard Elvis imitation, then you realize....Isaak is just that good. Nuff said.<br />  \\"Dixie Fried\\" ~ My favorite track. I've wanted Isaak to do a rock album ~ no ballads, just rock! ~ for years, and this track gives a hint of what that album could sound like. A musical party, Isaak's vocal is fun, fresh and fiesty, dancing over the rollicking rockabilly arrangement. (Once again, Plunkett just shines!). Love, love, LOVE it!!!<br />  \\"How's The World Treating You\\" ~ The song just doesn't fit Isaak, but a nice enough vocal, coupled with spot-on instrumentation.<br />  \\"It's Now Or Never\\" ~ Once again, the \\"cheese\\" factor is always a threat with this song, but Isaak brilliantly sidesteps the dairy and goes straight for romantic/dreamy/hypnotic. And that last note?!? MAN, O MAN!!!<br />  \\"Miss Pearl\\" ~ A kickin' rocker. Great vocal, even better guitar and organ!<br />  \\"Live It Up\\" ~ One of two originals. High energy, great vibe, relaxed, assured lead vocal. VERY nice.<br />  \\"I Walk The Line\\" ~ Earnest and honest, Isaak just grabs this baby by the tail and doesn't let go. Yes, it will always be one of Johnny's iconic signature songs, but right now? Right here? It's all Isaak's.<br />  \\"So Long I'm Gone\\" ~ There's just something about this cut that seems...shallow...and phoned in. One of the album's few weak tracks.<br />  \\"She's Not You\\" ~ This cut could have been on the radio 50-60 years ago...in other words, Isaak knocks this one out of the park! Romantic, seductive, infectious...a real winner.<br />  \\"My Happiness\\" ~ I keep going back and forth on this one...at times I think it's a bit calculated (the hiccup in the vocal), and I think it would have benefited from a stronger harmony vocal, but other listens..I like it. We'll call this one a draw for now!<br /> Disc 2 kicks off with:<br />\\"My Baby Left Me\\" ~ Arrangement, vocal, instrumentation...they all shine here. Sheer perfection.<br />  \\"Oh, Pretty Woman\\" ~ Another dangerous song to cover, but Isaak aces it. He doesn't overdo the growl, he doesn't try to be Roy...he just enjoys himself and the song. The result? By pulling things back and underplaying it (vs. Overplaying it, which most people do when covering this song!), it fits him like a glove!<br />  \\"Doin' The Best I Can\\" ~ Doo-wop heaven. Killer lead vocal, angelic backing vocals. Sweet!<br />  \\"Your True Love\\" ~ Not bad, but not amazing. Just sort of...O.K.<br />  \\"Crazy Arms\\" ~ Sort of has the same cookie cutter vibe as \\"Your True Love\\", but this track is saved by Plunkett's piano.<br />  \\"Lovely Loretta\\" ~ The second original, it's not as strong as \\"Live It Up\\", but I love the whistling and the little \\"Yeah!\\"<br />  \\"Everybody's In The Mood\\" ~ Another favorite. Totally swingin', everything just gels here...great boogie-woogie arrangement, smooth lead vocal, great harmony vocals, nice little touches (sweet harmonica!). Much like Rick Vito's excellent BAND BOX BOOGIE album, this cut has a classic feeling to it, but it also feels new and exciting. One of the prime examples of why BEYOND THE SUN works so well.<br />  \\"I'm Going To Sit Right Down And Cry\\" ~ Nice groove, but the vocal veers too close to Presleyland.<br />  \\"Love Me\\" ~ Another track that's a little too over the top. Love the backing vocals, though!<br />  \\"Doncha Think It's Time\\" ~ Has a nice lilt to it. Smooth, stylish, sublime.<br />  \\"That Lucky Old Sun\\" ~ The perfect album closer. Relaxed and bluesy, yet from the heart. Nice. Very, very nice.<br />  All in all, I have to say that BEYOND THE SUN is definiutely one of 2011's most pleasant surprises. Between this gem and 2009's equally strong MR. LUCKY, I can't wait to see what Chris Isaak does next. Rock on, Chris! (As with all my reviews, I'm docking the disc half a star, not for not including the lyrics as usual, but because I would have liked to have known why Isaak chose each of the songs. With the liner notes as extensive as they are, that's the one area we seem a little shortchanged!).\\"My Baby Left Me\\" ~ Arrangement, vocal, instrumentation...they all shine here. Sheer perfection.<br />  \\"Oh, Pretty Woman\\" ~ Another dangerous song to cover, but Isaak aces it. He doesn't overdo the growl, he doesn't try to be Roy...he just enjoys himself and the song. B00005YUAC|5.0|This is a great progressive rock album that was made before it was called progressive rock. This is a jazzy, trippy, rockin, trip into the 70's. A friend of mine in college bought this album because he thought the cover was cool and it was an expensive import. He hated the album, so he let me have it but he kept the cover. Thank God for CD technology because this version sounds so much better than that old LP did. The sound is mostly old fashioned Hammond Organ (Peter Bardens), trippy blues guitar (Andy Latimer), great bass playing by Doug Ferguson and Andy Ward is something special on the drums.<br /><br />\\"Slow Yourself Down\\" and \\"Mystic Queen\\" are a great way to start this trip back in time. \\"Six Ate\\" is one of the highlights with the whole band shining on this instrumental gem. \\"Separation\\" is just a simple rock and roll song, that is well done. \\"Never Let Go\\" is a Camel classic and the one that sticks in your head after the album is over. Just a great classic rock song.<br /><br />\\"Curiosity\\" is a blues influenced song that sounds a little like early Caravan. Latimer is a talented guitar player and with Bardens form a great pair that would go on to produce some of the best mellow rock ever played. I just love the stripped down sound of this song. \\"Arubaluba\\" finishes off the original LP and it is a Peter Bardens written jam. Andy Ward sticks out on this one and proves why he was considered a drum prodigy. Never let is be said that Camel couldn't rock.<br /><br />There are two bonus tracks: one is the single version of \\"Never Let Go\\" and is not really necessary, but the second bonus track is the gem of this package. \\"Homage To The God of Light\\" was recorded live in October of 1974 at the Marquee Club. Taken from a Peter Bardens solo album and turned into a 19 jam session. The quality of the recording is amazing. You will want to hear this one.<br /><br />This is my 50th review and it had to be a 5 star classic from one of my all time favorite bands. This isn't only for fans of progressive rock as it is a very timely early seventies sounding classic rock album, more than a progressive one. Why bother with titles, if you haven't heard this one: Why not?

B007Q2N0HA|4.0|I must admit, rather shamefully, that \\"Bared to You\\" is the first Sylvia Day book I've read, something I can't believe myself. How could I go so long without reading this seasoned, talented writer? Day's story enthralls and consumes the reader, and it does so well.<br /><br />Many a reviewer has already examined the plot of \\"Bared to You\\", so I'm skipping that to look at my favorite aspects of Day's writing. The story starts with Eva Tramell's recent move from San Diego to New York City--a wonderful introduction to both our heroine and setting. Consider the following passage from the novel's opening, \\"The smell of vehicle exhaust mixed with food from the vendor's carts, the shouts of hawkers blended with music from street entertainers, the awe-inspiring range of faces and styles and accents, the gorgeous architectural wonders... Real New Yorkers cruised right through it all, their love for the city was as comfortable and familiar as a favorite pair of shoes. They didn't view the steam billowing from potholes and vents in the sidewalks with romantic delight and they didn't blink an eye when the ground vibrated beneath their feet as the subway roared by below, while I grinned like an idiot and flexed my toes. New York was a brand new love affair for me.\\"<br /><br />I adore that line, \\"New York was a brand new love affair for me.\\" With that one sentence, Day's made the setting another character within her story.<br /><br />The story centers on Eva and Gideon, both characters with troubled pasts that influence significantly how they view and react to present-day relationships. Told from Eva's POV, the reader is privy to her inner thoughts, observations and interpretations of events. Lovely. But, unfortunately, we aren't afforded the same privilege with Gideon, which is such a shame. I love a story that lets me experience both of the main characters' POV, when I see the hero's more intimate side--especially when that hero is an Alpha Male--and witness how each event shifts the characters' feeling towards each other until they know they can't live without each other one minute longer.<br /><br />Still, as a fellow writer, I respect everything Day accomplished here. As soon as I finish this review I'm off to find another one.ts the characters' feeling towards each other until they know they can't live without each other one minute longer.<br /><br />Still, as a fellow writer, I respect everything Day accomplished here. 1932100660|5.0|We recently watched \\"Forks Over Knives\\" by happenstance on Netflix.  The movie was mindblowing for us and I had to know more about Dr. Campbell's research.  Let me start off by saying that we are not a family that eats steak every night of the week, which is why we were so blown away by this study.  My husband is a 20-year vegan and I am a 25-year vegetarian.  We are extremely conscious of what we eat, buying only organic produce, but allowing for some processed foods and plenty of olive oil to cook with.  I ate some cheese to flavor my food, had 1/2 and 1/2 in my coffee and ate milk chocolate, but avoided drinking milk and eating eggs.  We thought we were living healthy lives until.....I was just diagnosed with very early stage breast cancer.  I had always firmly believed that diseases like coronary artery disease and diabetes could be controlled by diet, but that cancer was something that was binary and gene related.  You were either going to get it or not.  Since no one in my family has died of cancer, I figured I was good on that front.  Wrong! I am only a few chapters into The China Study, but what I've read so far has just been mindblowing.  I literally can't stop thinking about it.  Everywhere I go, I think about it.  Because my husband was vegan, we ate a lot of margarine instead of butter and used a lot of olive oil to cook with.  That's all completely changed.  We've completely given up on added fats and are now eating more nuts and seeds, more avocados and olives.  Even for a vegetarian and a vegan, it's been a big challenge to figure out how to fry without oil, how to make a salad dressing without oil, what to spread on a piece of toast, etc, etc...but we're finding ways around using these old unhealthy products we'd been so copiously using in our diets.  We'd also been raising our three small kids as vegetarians believing that they needed the additional protein, B12 and calcium that came from dairy and eggs.  We are no longer doing that. We realize now that they can get all of those nutrients in a plant-based diet and take a B12 supplement daily.  The greatest gift we could give to our children is to start them off in life learning about what's healthy and what's not and how to make healthy choices in the way they approach and eat food.<br /><br />The China Study has made me realize that I've gone about my approach to eating in a slightly backward manner.  For years, I've focused on limiting external toxins, like pesticides, in the food I eat rather than paying attention to the food I was putting into my body.  I thought that because I was eating hormone-free, organic milk and cheese, it was healthy.  I've now refocused to what food I'm putting into my body rather than what's on it.  Like the rats in the dioxin study - I don't think any amount of pesticide on an apple even begins to approach the effect casein has on your body.<br /><br />If you're interested in living as healthy a life as possible and being educated about your food choices, do yourself a favor and buy this book and read it cover to cover.  Don't assume (like me) that you already know this stuff because chances are, you don't.  This book will in all likelihood be a life changer for you. B003AYPMCO|4.0|The \\"Deluxe\\" Edition of British Steel costs approximately twice as much as the [[ASIN:B003AYPMBU British Steel: 30th Anniversary Edition]] because you get \\"deluxe\\" packaging and a music CD that is the soundtrack to the DVD (Priest live at the Seminole Hard Rock Arena in Hollywood, Florida, August 2009). SO...if you want to listen to the live show in your car, or want to listen at home without watching, this is the package for you. There is no additional music when compared to the \\"standard\\" 30th Anniversary CD (the British Steel album) and DVD (the August 2009 live show).<br /><br />That said, it's a nice package to own...expensive or not...and I do happen to like the concert enough to take it with me in the car (and I'm too lazy to do a DVD audio rip for that purpose).<br /><br />I don't believe Priest has a \\"defining\\" album...they all have their moments, some more than others. And while British Steel was extremely popular (it spawned the concert classics Metal Gods, Breaking the Law, and Living After Midnight) as well as influential in the \\"New Wave Of British Heavy Metal,\\" you still can't overlook other classic albums like [[ASIN:B004IY6API Stained Class]], [[ASIN:B00005K9LK Screaming for Vengeance (Exp)]], [[ASIN:B0000630BT Painkiller]], and the live behemoth [[ASIN:B00005R62N Unleashed in the East (Exp)]].<br /><br />My guess is that the overwhelming majority of people reading this review already own British Steel in one form or another, and quite possibly some or all of the other albums I've named. So let's cut to the chase...the main draw here is the Hard Rock concert. Is it worth it? Yes, it is.<br /><br />There's a certain kind of power that Priest has acquired since Rob Halford's return on the [[ASIN:B0007MVXQG Angel Of Retribution]]. In the \\"old days,\\" the band was clawing its way to the top. These days, they have nothing to prove. The \\"Heavy Metal\\" genre of music owes its existence to the \\"big three\\"...Judas Priest, Iron Maiden, and Black Sabbath. Many people say that Sabbath invented the genre. Maybe they did, maybe they didn't. Tony Iommi is certainly the King of the Dark Riff. But Tony has his thing, and Glenn Tipton & K.K. Downing have theirs, and they are not the same thing. To fully grasp that, you need look no further than the jackhammer version of \\"Victim of Changes.\\" It's ironic that it's the crown jewel of the Hard Rock set because the track has nothing to do with British Steel...but it's a beast, a jaw-dropper. All of the elements of every other version are present, but the power...and the audience reaction...just unbelievable. Around the 6 minute mark, we get to the \\"Once she was wonderful, once she was mine...\\" section. At 7:32, as Halford's crooned \\"changessssssssssssssssssss\\" fades out, then Halford screams \\"VICTIM OF CHAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAANNNNNNNNNNNNNNNNNNNGGGGGGGGGGGGGGEEEEEEEEEEEEEEESSSSSSSSSSSS, and Tipton and Downing bring down the twin rhythm guitar thunder and you hear a mighty roar rise from the audience. At 7:45, Tipton just STANDS on it, just WRINGS that neck, leaning into one bent note after another as  it resolves back into twin rhythm guitar and a final climactic scream from Halford.<br /><br />Ozzy and Tony don't do it this way. Bruce Dickinson and company don't do it this way. There's only one band that can pull off a performance like this, and it's Judas Priest.<br /><br />So if you buy this (or the less expensive edition without the soundtrack CD), you'll get a prime classic Priest album and a contemporary live show that proves they've not lost a bit of power...where most bands would have slowed down and started phoning in their performances, Priest is still the beast unleashed.<br /><br />VERY highly recommended.

B004XJRQUQ|4.0|The first book of the trilogy is amazing. The post-apocalyptic future world is intriguing; the characters and relationships are well built. Once readers make it into the arena, there is non-stop action and narrative tension, making the book nearly impossible to put down. The violence is tastefully described and doesn't come across as only being there for shock value. (In many ways the book reminds me of the 2000 Japanese movie [[ASIN:B007MJSYF0 Battle Royale (English Subtitled)]], in which a class of high schoolers must battle to the death for the pleasure of their teacher.) The ending leaves a few questions unanswered and leaves you wanting more, excited to pick up the second book.<br /><br />Catching Fire fills in more information about the world of the novels and answers questions about how the Games function and the ruling political bodies who run them, showing Collins built the world on something more intricate than just random violence. It feels as though there could not possibly be as much captivating action, but Collins keeps the excitement pretty close to the height of the first book. There is a small feeling of repetitiveness regarding the Games, but Collins did a good job making it as different from the first book as possible.<br /><br />From the start, Mockingjay has a different feel from the first two books. Katniss is removed from most of the action, and although her trauma from the Games is realistic, reading about her many mental breakdowns is not nearly as exciting as reading about bloody violence. The entire book has the feeling of Collins writing it to send a message instead of entertaining readers and finishing the story. The love triangle is disturbed and resolved in an unsatisfactory way. Katniss becomes an irrelevant character. She functions as only an inspiring image for her people and her actions hardly effect the world or the war at all. This is an interesting idea, but makes for a boring main character. Katniss' character arc supports theoverarching theme of the novel that that war is senseless and nobody wins. I respect Collins for breaking the mold of this type of genre and having her characters react in such realistic, traumatized, ineffectual, and broken ways, but the reason why most authors don't do this is because it doesn't make for good narrative reading. By the end of the book when things are finally \\"resolved\\", having read this last book seems almost as pointless as war itself.

It is a recounting  of a personal journey in which Bryson is searching for the American towns he remembered from his childhood.  \\"I wanted to go back to the magic places of my youth-...and see if they were as good as I remembered them being\\"  Because he is searching for the magic and for the most part he is not finding it, there is a lot of negative thinking along the way. But we get to enjoy the ride and the wonderful, funny bits of nostalgia as he recounts bits of the many past family vacations, endearing us to his late father and perhaps finding bits of our own past in these stories. He is giving us his opinion of these places, sharing his feelings  through these humorous anecdotes as he ventures into the towns and cities along the way.  This was a lonely quest but we are allowed to share his thoughts about what he sees has happened to the country vs. his idealized vision from childhood.  The places never seem to have the \\"magic\\" and he is often irritated by this and by the tourists and unfriendly people he encounters. Ryan Murphy (responsible for another terrifically addictive FX show NIP/TUCK) and his co-showrunner and longtime colleague Brad Falchuk set out to create something that defies expectation of a horror story but keeps you in the game with its deceitfully addictive adult soap opera bits, all the while as parts of the show's mystery unravel as more questions are raised.<br /><br />What caused the house to be like this? Was it just the first murders, or was there something deeper? What does Constance really want with the Harmons? Does each new pope really get a message like the one presented in this show? Will Chad and Patrick ever get their perfect Halloween? Does Ben just REALLY like the stove?<br /><br />Actually, the best question is WHY DON'T THEY JUST LEAVE? They force a family member out of the house, and just a bit later, they force another family member to stay in the house. I'd hate to spoil anything here (other than Viv eating the brain, because that was awesome), but the answers are shocking. And what's even more important is that they work in the context of the show.<br /><br />The show is about truth and illusion, and coming from NIP/TUCK, Murphy and Falchuk are uniquely suited to do such a show. The illusion that this house will somehow save Ben and Vivien's too-fragile marriage as well as the eternally increasing distance from their teenage daughter Violet (who is already half a sociopath as the show starts). The truth is that the house, and its secrets, will destroy them the longer they stay; ultimately, the truth (no matter how painfully obvious it is) is not as powerful as the illusion.<br /><br />Another big part of this show's success is consulting producer and staff writer Tim Minear. Minear is a singular talent in genre entertainment. His ideas and his energy are always helping to branch out and make better whatever program he's working on. Sadly for Minear, all of the shows he's been a producer of (despite their pretty consistent level of excellence) have been cancelled. FIREFLY, STANDOFF, DRIVE, THE INSIDE, THE CHICAGO CODE, TERRIERS, WONDERFALLS... all ashes either during or just after their first seasons. His other shows like ANGEL and DOLLHOUSE actually lasted for at least two seasons, and AHS will join the ranks of those two shows, since it's been picked up for a second season on FX.<br /><br />Make no mistake: AMERICAN HORROR STORY is not a very good horror show, though. Is it creepy? Sure it is. But this show, aside from a few tingly moments (and that can go a few different ways since the show is pretty sexy at times too) doesn't offer much to actually scare you. It's more \\"How CRAZY can we make this show?\\" rather than \\"How SCARY can we make this show?\\" and that's problematic at times. B004XJRQUQ|1.0|After all the hype this series of books has gotten of late, I thought I would read the series objectively to see if they lived up to all of the praise and attention.  Unfortunately, it failed horribly on account of the author's lack of understanding of core literary concepts: character development, foreshadowing, and subtlety of plot devices.  I was actually mad at myself for taking time to read all three books in the series, but I felt like if I kept at it long enough, the series would end with the main character redeeming herself in some way, making her involvement in the story more than just a one-dimensional and emotionally distant girl with no personality.<br /><br />I've heard so many people defend the writing style of this book, saying that it's a \\"young adult\\" novel and as such should be held to a different set of standards.  I have two grievances with this: firstly, I have read plenty of other young adult novels, most of which side-step the spoon-feed approach to plot and character development that Suzanne Collins favors.  Secondly, it is insulting to young adults to insinuate that they're better suited to read stories that are not fully developed and do not use foreshadowing.  I don't know if the presumption is that they couldn't understand the subtleties of literature written like that, but I can say that when I was a young adult, I was capable of reading fully-developed literary works.  I have no reason to presume today's young adults are any less capable.<br /><br />This review will not touch on many plot points, partially because the stories were so limited in their ability to evoke emotions beyond boredom, frustration, or contempt for the characters, but mainly because there are many summaries of the stories available that focus only on plot details.  The only thing I wish to say about the plot is that, as the story develops over the 2nd and 3rd novels, the main character's thoughts and actions force you to hope and yearn for her to do something useful or redeeming.  About 3/4 the way through the 2nd novel, it becomes clear that the most meaningful thing Katniss could have done in the story would be to have died in some sacrificial way, using her death to somehow improve life for those that she presumably cares about (I say \\"presumably\\" because of her irritating habit of never allowing closeness to any other characters).  Needless to say, her death never comes, and ultimately Katniss never lives up to what little potential she had to truly affect the people she cared about in a positive way.  This should hardly be a spoiler; the novels are written in first-person, and I sincerely doubt Suzanne Collins is anywhere near skilled enough to pull of a postmortem first-person narrative.<br /><br />As the end of the 3rd novel came closer and closer, it had become increasingly clear by the number of pages left that the main character was not destined to evolve beyond what she was at the beginning of the series: a selfish, distant, moody caricature of the average 17-year-old girl.  I feel like I want to apologize to 17-year-old girls on behalf of Suzanne Collins if this is the way they've been summed up.<br /><br />Very seldom do I finish a single book without finding at least one or two redeeming qualities, but The Hunger Games Trilogy comes dangerously close.  The only thing that kept me reading the series was the concept of The Games: the compulsory gladiatorial contest to establish dominance over a people.  The idea was so repulsive that the desire to see how it panned out kept me interested in reading.  It's hard to imagine a group of people challenging such a horrible force in such a mundane way.<br /><br />I am not popular for my opinions about this series, but such as it is, I must stick by my opinion that the author is either incapable of writing with sophistication, or refuses to because she thinks little of her target audience.l or redeeming. They are connected to a bizarre, menacing cult called Sakigake whose leader (called Leader) engages in unspeakable acts that bring him to the attention of an acquaintance of Aomame's called the Dowager who runs a private organization that protects victims of domestic abuse.<br /><br />The book is slow--really slow--to develop.  Aomame and Tengo don't seem to be acquainted and it takes several hundred pages before you find out that the connection between them is that they knew each other when they were 10 years old, and once had a deeply meaningful hand-holding session in a classroom before Aomame's family moved away.  They have thought of and even yearned for each other over the 20 years since.<br /><br />Tengo is a math teacher and aspiring novelist whose editor friend persuades him to do a complete rewrite of a novel by a 17-year-old girl called Fuka-Eri.  Fuka-Eri is a beautiful, but odd and affectless girl.  Because of dyslexia, her writing is unskilled, but she has written a compelling story, called Air Chrysalis, about the Little People and their weaving work.  The connection of Tengo and Aomame to Sakigake--Tengo through Fuka-Eri and Aomame through the Dowager and Leader--will draw them closer to each other.<br /><br />So after almost 1000 pages (about 42 hours on audio), you'd figure you'd get an explanation of what the Little People, air chrysalises, Sakigake, and 1Q84 are all about, right?  Wrong!  Page after page of the same stories repeated, characters actually repeating each others' word over and over, banal descriptions of the food people prepare and eat, stunningly un-erotic descriptions of sex; that's what you get.  You also don't get much of a feeling for the purpose of entire secondary characters and their stories.  Aomame's friend Ayumi, for example.  She was an engaging personality, but she didn't seem to have much purpose except to have something happen to her that eerily mimics something that happened to Tengo's mother.  Though what happened to either woman doesn't seem to have much to do with the point of the book--whatever that point might be.<br /><br />And speaking of pointless, when I said that Tengo and Aomame are the principal narrators, that's because suddenly, in Book 3, a third narrator pops up.  Ushikawa is an investigator working for Sakigake and we have to read about his whole back story even though it has nothing to do with anything.  I finally figured out that the only reason why Ushikawa is a narrator at all is because he has a rather necessary scene late in the book with another secondary character without either Tengo or Aomame present.  Since neither of the narrators is present, another narrator became necessary and, therefore, we are burdened with many wasted pages of Ushikawa narrative.<br /><br />Haruki Murakami is practically a rock star among world novelists and it took five years from his previous book for 1Q84 to be published.  Breathless with anticipation, his many fans stampeded out to buy the book, making it an instant bestseller.  But sales dried up pretty quickly and not long after it was published you could get copies for cheap.  I got an audio from Audible for $4.99 (on sale, but still).  And if you read the positive reviews you notice that they're conceptual, rather than particularly descriptive.  The negative reviews are more concrete.  I think that's because you really have to cram a lot of your own meaning into this absurd and surrealistic story.  If you're not earnestly willing to do that, the clunky dialogue, constant repetition and ridiculous (and frequently distasteful) plot are just too much to overcome.<br /><br />I liked Aomame and Tengo and I kept going with the book to hear their story.  In the end, I think the story was about their belief in each other through many years and obstacles, and their making a new world for themselves once they found each other.  But all that Sakigake, Little People, air chrysalis, Fuka-Eri, Ushikawa and so much of the other stuff was all just a silly, repetitive, clunky waste of time to me.  The rewards of reading the book were tiny compared to the tedium and frustrations.  Only a hard-core Murakami fan will be interested or rewarded by this book.  They may say what Tengo's father says: \\"If you can't understand it without an explanation, you can't understand it with an explanation.\\"criptive.  The negative reviews are more concrete. 0307719219|5.0|Acemoglu and Robinson are well known in political science for their work on political institutions. Here, they argue that modernization theory has it backwards - good institutions cause economic growth, not the other way around. They focus on the need for political and economic institutions that allow property owners to invest in new technologies and products that result in \\"creative destruction.\\" In the absence of inclusive institutions, vested elites can stifle creative destruction in its cradle in order to protect the status quo.<br /><br />The book is obviously geared towards policymakers and the general public. There are no statistical tables for game theoretic models. The authors might have gone a bit too far in removing the \\"science\\" in political science, but the end result is a highly readable book. As such, the book probably won't satisfy political scientists, but that's not the point. I'd recommend the book for anybody already somewhat familiar with the broader concepts of modernization theory, but who also do not necessarily need or want formal political science methodology.

My complaint about the \\"odd plastic odor\\" was solved by the solution that I posted above, and I forgot about it until I read this review... no smell at all. I use the Kindle Fire daily, and there is no sign of wear or abrasion. It still functions beautifully, and though I've looked at other general use cases and covers over time, have found no compelling reason to switch to another type or brand. Kudos to Marware for making it right the first time.<br /><br />I do have a [[ASIN:B006G3FFJ6 Timbuk2 Kindle Fire Element Jacket]] for those days that I know I'm going to be out and about in bad weather, and for that specialized function, it's proving to be good... but it's not a replacement for my Marware Vibe Case. 0871404133|3.0|Never afraid of controversy, the Harvard sociobiologist Edward O. Wilson launched a bombshell in his 81st year.  In a coauthored paper of 2010, he disavowed what had been his trade-mark theory of kin selection (aka inclusive fitness) in favor of a new concept of eusocialization.  That is the central theme of this book.  Others more qualified than I will determine the fate of this courageous venture.<br /><br />The earlier chapters of this book are the best.  In these pages Wilson traces the twinned rise of two animal groups: insects (his field of specialization) and mammals, especially humans.  This account is readable and authoritative.  Yet the later chapters are a disappointment, to put it mildly.  Like many observers today, Wilson hopes to bridge the gap between science and the humanities.  Here he spectacularly fails.  He believes that in evolutionary terms religion started with the use of hallucinogenic drugs. This claim is bizarre and unprovable. As a scientist he should know better than to proffer speculation as truth.  As regards the visual arts, he uncritically buys into a current theory that prehistoric pictographs (where nothing is known about the belief systems of the creators) are the product of shamanic visions.  Moreover, he thinks that a simplistic theory of pattern recognition is responsible for our response to art.  A glance at the Paul Gauguin painting on the book's cover should suffice to scuttle this claim.<br /><br />I do not know what the fertility of Wilson's new approach will prove to be in the natural sciences.  In the humanities, though, it is a dud.  It remains to be seen whether others can do better.

0393064476|3.0|Once upon a time there was an English major who came across the poem \\"On the Nature of Things,\\" by Lucretius.  He really liked this poem and decided to write an essay about how it influenced the development of the Renaissance by reintroducing humanist and Epicurean ideals that had been suppressed by the church for centuries.  The essay grew and turned into a book. The resulting book has a fine and interesting thesis, but in trying to prove his point the author goes too far.  Suddenly, a huge amount of the Renaissance flowering is due to the rediscovery of this glorious poem and the church is portrayed in an increasingly evil light for the Greek ideals it has repressed.  This ignores many other major world events which shaped the Renaissance such as the collapse of Constantinople and the resulting influx of intellectuals from there that spread knowledge and humanist ideals across Europe.  Not a bad book, but like an overextended essay it takes a single idea and stretches it way too far.

One thing I love about this band is that they can change their style on each record but they still sound like Evanescence.  I do miss the epic strings and choirs that were used in their other works. The strings are used sparsely and choirs have been basically cut or replaced with Amy's layered vocals. But this album is fun and addictive once you get used to the energetic new sound. The lyrics are also a lot more positive and optimistic than previous albums. Most of the songs use the same basic guitar and drum sounds, with added occasional experimentation. I did welcome the addition of harp into the music, Amy plays it wonderfully and it gives a few of the songs a storybook atmosphere.<br /><br />I haven't had a chance to see this material performed live yet but I bet it's a blast. Amy sings each one amazingly, especially when she hits the high note at the end of the first.<br /><br />I approve of the synths used in Oceans. It's the only song other than Secret Door that isn't intense or guitar driven.<br /><br />Bonus tracks: New Way To Bleed: Definitely should have been used in the standard. Secret Door: Harp, strings and Amy's vocals dominate most of this song and I adore it. This sounds like something right out of a fairy tale. 0307352145|5.0|Are you an introvert like me who has ever wondered if there is something wrong with being the way you are? Quiet, by Susan Cain, offers a well-researched book about what makes you the way you are from the culture you grew up in to your genetics. American society, in particular, places a lot of value on being extroverted and it proves to be true in a lot of business environments.  Sadly, a lot of talent and vision is overlooked due to the overvaluation of extroverts.<br /><br />Cain does an excellent job of covering the history of why extroversion has become such a valued trait in some cultures, what introverts can do to succeed without changing who they are and even how to do your best to help an introverted child be comfortable in their own skin.<br /><br />While I enjoyed this book very much, for me, the amount of research information was overwhelming at times. However, I would recommend this book to anyone, both introverts seeking to understand their genetic and cultural introvert makeup and to extroverts trying to understand the other 1/3 of the population.<br /><br />I received this book for free from Blogging for Books in exchange for my review.
